# COBOL's Influence on C

_150 messages · 20 participants · 2007-11_

---

### COBOL's Influence on C

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
- **Date:** 2007-11-05T11:02:50-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<13iufoh3m3nh7a0@corp.supernews.com>`

```

From an ISO draft technical document:
-----
ISO/IEC JTC1 SC22 WG14 N1176
Date: 2006-05-24
Reference number of document: ISO/IEC WDTR 24732
Committee identification: ISO/IEC JTC1 SC22 WG14
SC22 Secretariat: ANSI
Information Technology -
Programming languages, their environments and system software interfaces -
Extension for the programming language C to support decimal floating-point
arithmetic -
---
Even though the hardware may not provide decimal arithmetic
operations, the support can still be emulated by software.
Programming languages used for business applications either
have native decimal types (such as PL/I, COBOL, C#, or
Visual Basic) or provide decimal arithmetic libraries (such as
the BigDecimal class in Java). The arithmetic used, nowadays,
is almost invariably decimal floating-point; the COBOL 2002
ISO standard, for example, requires that all standard decimal
arithmetic calculations use 32-digit decimal floating-point.
-----
```

On 11/05/07 11:02 am, Rick Smith wrote:
> From an ISO draft technical document:
> -----
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> arithmetic calculations use 32-digit decimal floating-point.

I don't intend to get into a debate about the pros/cons of various 
internal representations of data, nor how they are processed in the 
languages.  The following is simply to state a few facts that expands on 
the above:
1)  IBM System z [mainframe systems] do have hardware implementation of 
decimal float.
2)  Support for the hardware in #1 is provided for programs written in 
[in alphabetical order]:
     a) Assembler Language
     b) C/C++
     c) PL/I
     d) The SHARE User Group has requested support for COBOL,
        but IBM has not yet responded to the requirement.

Carl
```

```
On Nov 5, 4:02 pm, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:
> From an ISO draft technical document:
> -----
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> -----

The COBOL 2002 version of standard arithmetic is being superceded by
two IEEE754r compliant forms called standard binary arithmetic and
standard decimal arithmetic.  The new IEEE754r is still in draft, but
now seems fairly stable, at least in so far as J4 COBOL plans to
implement it.  As I understand it from J4 dccuments, no one has yet
implemented the standard ariihmetic of the 2002 COBOL standard and, in
view of the forthcoming changes, it is extremely unlikely that any
will. Whether or not they implement the new forms remains to be seen,
I believe that IEEE754r recommends that at least standard decimal
arithmetic be implemented by programming languages.

As a practical expedient, COBOL will diverge slightly from full
compliance from IEEE754r. The default rounding form for the new types
of standard arithmetic will still be done in the traditional COBOL
manner, presumably and reasonably for backward compatibility.
Programmers can still get effective compliance with IEEE754r by
explicitly defining the rounding mode.
```

```
"Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net> wrote:
>
> I f they really want to improve C they should add an EVALUATE statement.

AFAIK, C has always had the 'switch' statement, Pascal has the 'case'
statement, modern BASICs have the SELECT statement, etc., all of which
predated EVALUATE, and from which I suspect EVALUATE was taken.
Unlike the other languages, C's 'switch' statement 'drops through' successive
'case' phrases. EVALUATE is more powerful (flexible) than the others;
perhaps that was what you meant. :-)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL's Influence on C

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
- **Date:** 2007-11-12T10:19:38-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<13jgrs7k1nqet88@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<13iufoh3m3nh7a0@corp.supernews.com> <1194781359.463558.183080@v2g2000hsf.googlegroups.com> <13je1inhintak13@corp.supernews.com> <13je7n9dih2jtdd@corp.supernews.com> <gYHZi.2085$2n.224@bignews8.bellsouth.net>`

```

"Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote in message
news:gYHZi.2085$2n.224@bignews8.bellsouth.net...
> "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net> wrote:
> >
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> predated EVALUATE, and from which I suspect EVALUATE was taken.

Microsoft QuickBASIC's SELECT CASE appears
to date from about 1987. COBOL's EVALUATE was
in the 1985 standard; but was first recommended by the
CODASYL COBOL Committee in 1978. I suspect that
1978 predates every concept of "modern BASICs".

Both C's switch and Pascal's case have labels.
FORTRAN's computed GOTO and COBOL's GO TO
DEPENDING have labels and predate both C's switch
and Pascal's case. Each of the languages: FORTRAN,
COBOL, C, and Pascal, use, similarly, an integral value
to transfer control to a label.

COBOL's EVALUATE uses conditions, instead of labels.
Conditions and labels are not the same despite the
appearance of similarity in some circumstances.

I suspect that EVALUATE was designed to accommodate
decision tables, to include a capability to replace some uses
of GO TO DEPENDING, and that other programming
languages had no influence in regard to EVALUATE.

Modern BASICs also use conditions in the SELECT CASE
statement and may have been influenced by COBOL. <g>
```

```
Rick Smith wrote:

> 
> 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Both C's switch and Pascal's case have labels.

The BLISS language has SELECT and SELECTONE.  SELECT will match as many 
subsections as possible, SELECTONE has a 'break' at the end of each 
subsection.  There are also ALWAYS and OTHERWISE sections which can 
appear more than once in the list of subsections.  All allow full 
run-time expressions, not just compile-time case labels.  So you can do 
SELECT True OF; 'case over strings'; etc.  Plus SELECT[ONE] returns a 
value so you can even use it as an expression (all BLISS statements are 
really expressions.) Circa 1969 at CMU.

All taken together does pretty much what EVALUATE does (although EVALUTE 
is slightly more flexible).

I'm certain that COBOL wasn't influenced by BLISS.  Sometimes smart 
people arrive at the same solution independently.
```

On Nov 13, 12:23 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:

> >Modern BASICs also use conditions in the SELECT CASE

Cobol added nested programs which are exactly 'a local function call'.
If this had _replaced_, ie removed, the out of line PERFORM then
almost 100% of Cobol programs would have been broken.  That would have
destroyed Cobol faster than anything else.

What MS did with VB.NET is irrelevant because there was no continuum
of VB to VB.NET, they did different things.

- **From:** "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net>
- **Date:** 2007-11-13T12:47:57-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Imm_i.4708$LZ7.1327@newsfe15.lga>`
- **References:** `<13iufoh3m3nh7a0@corp.supernews.com> <1194781359.463558.183080@v2g2000hsf.googlegroups.com> <13je1inhintak13@corp.supernews.com> <13je7n9dih2jtdd@corp.supernews.com> <gYHZi.2085$2n.224@bignews8.bellsouth.net> <13jgrs7k1nqet88@corp.supernews.com> <dsmhj317b7236kep9l7j2i53jp39dcvlo6@4ax.com>`

```

Robert <no@e.mail> wrote in message
news:dsmhj317b7236kep9l7j2i53jp39dcvlo6@4ax.com...
> On Mon, 12 Nov 2007 10:19:38 -0500, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
wrote:
>
> >Modern BASICs also use conditions in the SELECT CASE
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> GOSUB, which is the same as Cobol's out of line PERFORM, has been
depreciated by many/most
> compilers and dropped from VB.NET.  It was replaced by SUB, which is a
local function
> call. In my opinion, Cobol should have done the same.

In my opinion and usage, the word SUB, short for SUBroutine, refers to a
stand-alone piece of code not included with the source program under
consideration.  (It can certainly be in the same compilation unit, of
course).  A function in this context is some common routine which isn't
compiled (I say this to keep OO vocabulary out of the discussion), usually
supplied by the compiler writers: e.g., calls on the OS.  There should be a
completely different verb to execute sections of code within the source
program as opposed to that used to execute wholly external sections.
COBOL's PERFORM does the first perfectly well - unambiguous,
understandable - and CALL (or INVOKE, etc.) does the second: and neither can
be mistaken for the other.  Why change?

In PROGRESS, a language I have little love for but have had to use quite a
bit, the in-line perform is WHILE, and the out-of-line perform is RUN: but
RUN is also used as the CALL.  If the label isn't defined within the current
source, it's assumed to be an external routine: no precautionary diagnostic.
So it's very common to have a clean compile which then blows up with
unresolved linkages when attempting to execute.  Not the same situation as
what Robert's suggesting but a cautionary tale.

PL
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL's Influence on C

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>
- **Date:** 2007-11-13T18:18:09-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<snckj3564s4rjul5ctvffht6imaih84m4q@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<13iufoh3m3nh7a0@corp.supernews.com> <1194781359.463558.183080@v2g2000hsf.googlegroups.com> <13je1inhintak13@corp.supernews.com> <13je7n9dih2jtdd@corp.supernews.com> <gYHZi.2085$2n.224@bignews8.bellsouth.net> <13jgrs7k1nqet88@corp.supernews.com> <dsmhj317b7236kep9l7j2i53jp39dcvlo6@4ax.com> <Imm_i.4708$LZ7.1327@newsfe15.lga>`

```
On Tue, 13 Nov 2007 12:47:57 -0600, "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net> wrote:

>
>Robert <no@e.mail> wrote in message
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
>be mistaken for the other.  Why change?

The target of a Cobol CALL can be in the same source or it can be external.

>In PROGRESS, a language I have little love for but have had to use quite a
>bit, the in-line perform is WHILE, and the out-of-line perform is RUN: but
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>what Robert's suggesting but a cautionary tale.

Cobol, along with most compiled languages, is the same. Depending on linker options, you
learn of missing external names either at link or execution time. 

Unlike most compiled languages, Cobol lets you CALL a name stored in a variable. That kind
can only be bound at execution time. The calling program can handle missing names with an
ON condition, but it's usually too late to do anything but abort. I've never really needed
that feature. It is a bad idea for two reasons. First, it usually slows every call, not
just the first. Second, the linker/loader nor tools like ldd cannot check those references
because they don't know what value will be in the variable at execution time. It's usually
a constant, but there's no way to declare that.

On Unix, you can check for missing externals by running "ldd -r (program)', both after
compilation and before execution. Do z/OS or CA-7 have that ability?
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ao5nj3htd2qp1g6p1r7r2c8pvmp8556ul5@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<13iufoh3m3nh7a0@corp.supernews.com> <1194781359.463558.183080@v2g2000hsf.googlegroups.com> <13je1inhintak13@corp.supernews.com> <13je7n9dih2jtdd@corp.supernews.com> <gYHZi.2085$2n.224@bignews8.bellsouth.net> <13jgrs7k1nqet88@corp.supernews.com> <dsmhj317b7236kep9l7j2i53jp39dcvlo6@4ax.com> <Imm_i.4708$LZ7.1327@newsfe15.lga> <snckj3564s4rjul5ctvffht6imaih84m4q@4ax.com> <1195030556.119704.154360@q5g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Wed, 14 Nov 2007 00:55:56 -0800, Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Nov 14, 1:18 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
>> On Tue, 13 Nov 2007 12:47:57 -0600, "tlmfru" <la...@mts.net> wrote:
…[55 more quoted lines elided]…
>exploit it to build dynamic systems does not make it bad.

This issue requires a rant on the inadequacies of programming language designers. They
cause UNINTENDED CONSEQUENCES because they think like programmers rather administrators.
Two examples are fresh in my mind because I'm fighting with them right now. 

Picture a very large shop that runs hundreds of thousands of batch jobs that execute
millions of programs every month. Imagine an administrator writing automated
'verification' of those jobs BEFORE they run, so errors can be fixed BEFORE the jobs blow
up in production. The existance of data files is the easiest thing to check. What about
the existance of program files (verifying they are the correct version is the next step)?

Unix's ELF standard and the aforementioned ldd (LoaDer Diagnostics) tool makes it easy to
verify the existance of called programs, recursively checking for ALL of them. It works
for every language EXCEPT Cobol (and other programs calling dlopen() directly, which is
rare). Why do Cobol programmers need the flexibility to determine the names of child
programs at execution time? In the vast majority of cases, they don't. A few creative
programmers will write  'dynamic systems' driven by decision tables (or similar) stored in
databases, from which they get the names of child programs. That allows them to add child
programs without having to recompile the parents .. provided someone installs the child
program BEFORE putting its name into the database. That's fine for small systems with a
small number of users. The programmer will personally install the child program. It
doesn't work when there are dozens to hundreds of  machines. Worse, the list of child
programs is in a NON-STANDARD location unknown to a general verification tool. Each
creative programmer will design a DIFFERENT location. One might put the list in a load
module, one in a database, one on his laptop's floppy mounted via NFS. 

This is anarcy. In order to verify programs, I need ALL externals stored in ELF headers.
For Cobol programs calling via variables (data-names), I have to extract the VALUE from
the data-name definition, look for MOVE 'prognm' TO DATA-NAME, use the values to build a
dummy object that gets linked into the executable so the program's child names are in its
symbol table. THIS SHOULD NOT BE NECESSARY. If someone on the Cobol Standards Committee
had known the requirements of LARGE systems, the language would make a distinction between
dynamic binding (a good thing) and dynamic determination of called program name (a bad
thing). 

------

My second gripe is over nested COPYs. The '85 Cobol Standard says they are not allowed.
The unintended consequence of that stupid restriction is that our builds take 18 hours
rather than 2 hours. Here's why. We don't write copybooks manually, we generate them from
data dictionaries, for several languages -- .h files for C, .cpy for Cobol. We produce
separate copybooks for 88 levels and for types. A Cobol type looks like "pic x(10)". The
top level copybook for a structure looks like:
     05  customer-number            copy custno.typ..
     05  customer-status              copy status.typ..
           copy status.val.            *> contains 88 levels

Because the '85 Cobol Standard says nested COPYs are a no-no, we run a pre-pre-processor
that combines the nested COPYs into a single one, which looks like this:
custrow.cpy:
    05  customer-number               pic x(10).
    05  customer-status                pic  x(01).
          88  active   value 'A'.
          88  cancelled value 'C'.

Our compilations are driven by make files, which are driven by generated dependency (make
rule) files, which are created from source code. The problem is, we cannot determine the
dependencies from reading Cobol source programs. All we see is "copy custrow.cpy". We
cannot tell that status.val is a dependent. (Make decides what to compile by comparing
timestamps of dependents to the timestamp of the executable.)  One way to make it work
would be to generate custrow.cpy into a temp area, compare it to the previous custrow.cpy,
replacing custrow.cpy only if there is a difference. THIS SHOULD NOT BE NECESSARY,
especially since Micro Focus and the '02 Cobol Standard both support nested COPYs. A
better way to make it work would be to simply eliminate the pre-pre-processor. Instead we
run 18 hour full builds because somebody back in the early '80s thought nested COPYs were
too much trouble or unnecessary. They WERE unnecessary for small systems. If someone on
the '85 Standards Committee had known the requirements of LARGE systems (10,000s of
programs), our builds would already be running in 2 hours.
```

Excuse me, but you dismiss the idea that data file names are in data
items in the program and may be built arbitrarily by the program (as I
in fact do with all my systems) and then rant on about how the same
thing for programs might be a problem.

> What about
> the existance of program files (verifying they are the correct version is the next step)?

Actually you need to start with the job scripts, REXX scripts and
whatever else can start a binary executable, including system() calls.
You are merely ignoring real world in attempting to 'prove' that your
rant has any substance at all.

> Why do Cobol programmers need the flexibility to determine the names of child
> programs at execution time? In the vast majority of cases, they don't.

That may be. But dynamic calls are not just so one can 'make up' a
program name. In most cases the names are not arbitrary but are in
menu files or system control. In an interactive system the user is
presented with a range of options to select from which is the menu
file. On selecting a particular operation the particular program used
may depend on who the user is or what system options are in force.

> A few creative
> programmers will write  'dynamic systems' driven by decision tables (or similar) stored in

Yes it does work. For a system all programs are issued and the menu or
control file establishes which are valid for a particular user or
system option. In fact with my systems it also does access level and
password control.

> Worse, the list of child
> programs is in a NON-STANDARD location unknown to a general verification tool.

You have simply 'made up' a hypothetical so-called 'general'
verification tool which is inadequate and is not 'general' at all and
then criticized the design of it.  Well, duh, do better next time.

> Each

Only is situations where you have advocated the removal of 'shop

>
> This is anarcy. In order to verify programs, I need ALL externals stored in ELF headers.

'Need' all you want. I need other things and I care not about what you
want.

Dynamic calls are easily checked prior to the call or excepted via ON
EXCEPTION by the program itself. Well mine certainly do that and I
don't know any system that I have worked on that didn't.

> For Cobol programs calling via variables (data-names), I have to extract the VALUE from
> the data-name definition, look for MOVE 'prognm' TO DATA-NAME, use the values to build a
> dummy object that gets linked into the executable so the program's child names are in its
> symbol table. THIS SHOULD NOT BE NECESSARY.

I really don't care whether you use dynamic loading or not. It solves
problems for me and for many others. Problems such as isolating
changes so that module testing can be done and new routines issued
without having to relink and retest all programs. In fact the problem
of not relinking and thus leaving an old routine in production buried
in a static linked program is worse that your nonsense arguments.

> If someone on the Cobol Standards Committee
> had known the requirements of LARGE systems, the language would make a distinction between
> dynamic binding (a good thing) and dynamic determination of called program name (a bad
> thing).

That is only your opinion that it is 'bad'. In fact many modern
languages use reflection.

I suppose that you would also claim that building file names from,
say, company indicator and function was also 'bad' because your
'verification' cannot know this.


> My second gripe is over nested COPYs. The '85 Cobol Standard says they are not allowed.
> The unintended consequence of that stupid restriction is that our builds take 18 hours
…[28 more quoted lines elided]…
> programs), our builds would already be running in 2 hours.

Excuse me, but nested COPYs would _not_ solve the problem because if
you changed a 2nd level that was in several 1st level COPY books then
your dependency building should (is required to) find that and thus be
equivalent to all the 1st levels being changed.

Alternately, only do your pre-pre-processing based on a make of the
1st level COPY and don't rebuild everything regardless of dependecies.

The problem isn't COBOL it is the stupid way that you build your
copybooks.

m, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
> >> On Wed, 14 Nov 2007 00:55:56 -0800, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> easy to verify mainframe jobs.

```
On Fri, 16 Nov 2007 23:42:50 -0800 (PST), Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Nov 17, 7:40 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:

>> >> We get many complaints from legitimate users; bad guys never call for support.
>>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>jobs are 'aborting more often' in other environments ?

The references for job failure rate can be:

1. Industry averages/norms. 
2. Benchmarks against similar operations.
3. Cost/benefit analysis.
4. Shop history. 
5. Personal experience with similar operations.

I've worked at places that averaged one failure per month, and places that averaged a
hundred failures per month  .. for applications of comparable size and complexity. The
former got it down by doing things like validating jobs.

Most causes of failure are unrelated to Cobol. I brought up the dynamic call problem in
this forum because it is specific to Cobol.  Responses explain why that's so -- Cobol
programmers don't understand the problem, nor how Cobol causes it. That's what I meant
when I said "think like programmers rather than administrators."

>> >> Here is an account of job verification from a shop smaller than mine. Note especially
>> >> "requested module not found." You should write to the website's owner with the news that
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>the batch ran why is it not there now ?

A bad library path is one common reason, as I said earlier.

Another is the installation of new third party software. A typical call hierarchy is not
as simple as MAIN calling A, B and C, it is usually MAIN calling A, which calls B from a
third party, which calls C from another third party. Now B is replaced. The new B requires
a new C. On the development machine, C _is_ replaced (by a developer, after a test
aborts). Due to missed communication, C is not replaced on the production machine.
```

```
On Sat, 17 Nov 2007 13:06:05 -0800 (PST), Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Nov 18, 9:37 am, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
>> On Fri, 16 Nov 2007 23:42:50 -0800 (PST), Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote:
…[28 more quoted lines elided]…
>problem because it _isn't_ a problem to them.

They must not work in large shops that run 100,000 jobs per month. I thought mainframers
did.

>You did _not_ 'establish' that they 'don't understand how Cobol causes
>it', which is just more of your self-promoting puffery, it just is not
>a problem.

Best I can tell, z/OS has no tool like ldd to check whether called programs exist. I think
the reason is because they run so much Cobol, which cannot be checked. If they ran mostly
C and a little Cobol, like Unix, they'd have such a tool. Then they'd understand that
Cobol causes a problem.  

>> >What is making the programs 'disappear' from the machine such that
>> >suddenly it cannot be found ?  If a program was there last week when
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>What causes the 'library path' to change ?  

Typical Unix applications run an 'environment script' that's 'sourced in' (included) from
a remote location controlled by systems guys, not applications guys. The library path is
set in that script. It's not set once for the whole installation, it's set for each
application system.  For example, payroll might be different from general ledger.

Deployment to production is controlled by a third group: change management (where I often
work). Problems occur because of miscommunication between systems guys, who change the
environment script, and change management guys, who deploy executables. 

>How would showing that the
>called program is in its proper place by a verification routine solve
>bad programmers writing bad library paths. 

Applications programmers don't write library paths; systems programmers write library
paths. The exception is development environments, aka sandboxes, where applications
programmers can do anything they want. When (not if) they find a path is screwed up, they
fix it. But they often don't communicate the fix to systems programers. Similarly, when
they find a third party library is missing, they remedy the problem in their sandbox, but
don't communicate that to change (deployment) managers. 

>If a job has a bad library path and a verification is done after the
>path is set then the job will fail. How is this different than the
>program starting and failing immediately because it can't find its
>called program, or dll ?

The difference is hours of time to fix the problem before the program runs for real.

ALso, the failing program might be deep into a jobstream that runs for hours. Depending on
restart procedures, recovery might have to rerun many other programs. 

>> Another is the installation of new third party software. A typical call hierarchy is not
>> as simple as MAIN calling A, B and C, it is usually MAIN calling A, which calls B from a
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>That is simple to detect by do a diff on the structure listing. 

What's a structure listing, and how do I get one?

>I would also suggest that the program date/time be compared because
>even if the program _is_ there it may have been updated in system test
>and not in production. This applies regardless of language or how the
>calls are made and is a fairly basic check when moving to
>production.

Timestamps are an obsolete idea we stopped using in the '90s. We now use version numbers,
which tie directly into the version control (change management) system. You DO use
version control, like CVS or SVN, don't you?

Unix lets you put the version number into the ELF header. That's how you check the version
deployed. That's also how you check the version of third party software, and other third
party software called by it. 

>Is the above something you were responsible for and and now try to
>blame others ?

Ad hominem ignored, as usual.
```

>else's fault.

>> >How would showing that the
>> >called program is in its proper place by a verification routine solve
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>of Cobol, or language designers.

How?

>> >If a job has a bad library path and a verification is done after the
>> >path is set then the job will fail. How is this different than the
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>path tested is the one in the production run.

1. The loader does a checksum to verify the program is unchanged.
2. The version number ties back to system test. 
3. Verification runs on the production machine. 

>> ALso, the failing program might be deep into a jobstream that runs for hours. Depending on
>> restart procedures, recovery might have to rerun many other programs.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>production systems.

"Do a diff" is an inadequate brushoff answer. There are hundreds of thousands of files on
the machine, most of which are unrelated to the application. We do compare version numbers
on the production machine to version numbers from system test. 

>> >> Another is the installation of new third party software. A typical call hierarchy is not
>> >> as simple as MAIN calling A, B and C, it is usually MAIN calling A, which calls B from a
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>ls -alR base directory >listing

That's not a "structure listing", it's an inventory of files, most of which are irrelevant
to the application. I mocked simple-minded answers like this when I wrote "NOW why didn't
you run my payroll job?" 

>> >I would also suggest that the program date/time be compared because
>> >even if the program _is_ there it may have been updated in system test
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>systems and run a diff on these.

That's what we do.

>If a file is missing or has the wrong version then diff will tell you.

We do it only for top level executables, not called programs. A basic conceptual flaw is
the notion that A Program is a single executable. We want ELF headers to tell us about
child programs. They do for all except Cobol.

>> Unix lets you put the version number into the ELF header. That's how you check the version
>> deployed. That's also how you check the version of third party software, and other third
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>Cobol, or Call dataname, or ...

Ad hominem translates to  "against the person". In general, there are two ways of dealing
with problems. One approach is to identify the guilty INDIVIDUAL and punish him. For
example by calling him stupid or isolating him from the herd. It doesn't solve the
problem, but it makes us feel vindicated. We tell ourselves making one person 'an example'
will serve as a deterrent. History shows that approach simply doesn't work. 

We do it because we're programmed at the emotional level to solve problems that way. Our
emotions can ONLY deal with people. They cannot deal with abstract problems. The only way
they can solve abstract problems is by 'personalizing' them, i.e. investing them with a
personality. 

Consumers do it with brand names, e.g. 'I hate Microsoft' and 'I love BMWs'.  In business,
the process of converting a problem into a person is called 'accountability'. 

The rational approach is to deal with the problem directly. Not to invest it with a
personality so we can pretend it's a person and hand it off to the emotional decision
process.
```

```
On Sun, 18 Nov 2007 21:59:31 -0800 (PST), Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Nov 19, 4:07 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
>> On Sun, 18 Nov 2007 10:51:08 -0800 (PST), Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote:
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>in fact, have that problem"

No. I didn't have time.


>> CVS does not know about call relationships, and we cannot assume every program in the
>> repository is required.
>
>Yet you do seem to assume that everything in the ldd list is required.

It is. The Unix loader will fail a program with missing components, even if they're called
from dead code. You can eliminate dead code with he strip utility. 

>> >Well, you are quite wrong about that.  You won't see an ELF header on
>> >(most) Java, PERL, Python, PHP, and many others.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>languages can be compiled to ELF binaries.

The four you mentioned can be. The PERL native code compiler, ccperl, comes with the
standard package. 

>> Calling via data name is a technical issue. Many programmrs don't understand why it causes
>> an administrative problem, which is easily avoided by calling via literal.
>
>While you don't seem to understand the benefits of calling by
>dataname. Benefits not available with call literal.

The only benefit is not having to recompile the parent when adding a child. If the child
is non-trivial, there will be logic changes to the parent anyway.

> >> >Well, you are quite wrong about that.  You won't see an ELF header on
> >> >(most) Java, PERL, Python, PHP, and many others.

> >> They all can be compiled to native code executables.

> >languages can be compiled to ELF binaries.
>
> The four you mentioned can be. The PERL native code compiler, ccperl, comes with the
> standard package.

'ccperl' is _NOT_ a native compiler for Perl, it is a Perl script
interpreter with 'ClearCase' incorporated. It isn't in the 'standard
package' and is for Windows.

Python does give .pyc file which are byte coded, not ELF binaries.
There are also JIT Python compilers which do not produce ELF binaries.

The PHP 'compilers' that I have seen do not produce 'native code' but
simply wrap an embedded interpreter and the script into a binary file.



> >> Calling via data name is a technical issue. Many programmrs don't understand why it causes
> >> an administrative problem, which is easily avoided by calling via literal.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> is non-trivial, there will be logic changes to the parent anyway.

See, I told you that you didn't understand.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL's Influence on C

_(reply depth: 28)_

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>
- **Date:** 2007-11-19T12:46:04-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<oel3k3hdac3i5koh6okbqh5sdlorofeerb@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<a8huj31adgq5mkghr8spkbj101umcpukr2@4ax.com> <7rb2k3hsv27p3np930b87oq2gt8blrhe3n@4ax.com> <fhrnsf$fbm$1@reader1.panix.com> <q843k310idth5jp9i4qtioqq0uf31dt150@4ax.com> <fhs6n9$1ng$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
On Mon, 19 Nov 2007 14:31:05 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:

>In article <q843k310idth5jp9i4qtioqq0uf31dt150@4ax.com>,
>Robert  <no@e.mail> wrote:
…[32 more quoted lines elided]…
>anyway.'

I said "when ADDING a child," meaning new child not called before. You described replacing
a child with one of the same name.

>>Thank you for
>>confirming my point that Cobol programmers don't understand the difference.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>choices than anything else.

There is no reason to put your called program's name in data name, because it never
changes. You can get late binding two ways:

01  C01-TURBOTAX-NAME  VALUE 'TURBOTAX"  PIC X(8).
    CALL C01-TURBOTAX-NAME USING ....

DYNAM
    CALL "TURBOTAX" USING ....

In both cases you can replace TURBOTAX without recompiling the calling program.

The difference is that the second method tells general purpose tools that your program
will be calling TURBOTAX, so they can verify its presence.

On Unix, you don't have to say DYNAM because late binding is the defrault and the norm.
You must specify options to get static binding, which is seldom used.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL's Influence on C

_(reply depth: 20)_

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>
- **Date:** 2007-11-18T12:20:38-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ffu0k3prsa4hdhloenm5etrhnvvau49n34@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<ao5nj3htd2qp1g6p1r7r2c8pvmp8556ul5@4ax.com> <a8huj31adgq5mkghr8spkbj101umcpukr2@4ax.com> <fc1ffd1f-ca85-4c2e-adf4-087a597a7150@b40g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <v55vj3poupt4b7nn5fqnpabg68dqlahbeo@4ax.com> <fhpi09$q4v$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
On Sun, 18 Nov 2007 14:25:13 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:

>In article <v55vj3poupt4b7nn5fqnpabg68dqlahbeo@4ax.com>,
>Robert  <no@e.mail> wrote:
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
>communicate is at fault.

The best way to verify all software components are in place is an automated tool. We use
such a tool for everything EXCEPT programs called by Cobol.

The fallback solution is communication between people, in the form of component lists.
Like most human based solutions, it is not 100% foolproof. The lists don't cover third
party libraries nor environment scripts. They can't, because the test environment is not
EXACTLY the same as the production environment. An infrequently called program can also
slip through the cracks, if and only if it's not listed as an external AND is missing from
the list.

So the answer to your question is that BOTH the language and the people are at fault when
a program is missing.
```

```
On Sun, 18 Nov 2007 18:57:18 -0600, Robert <no@e.mail> wrote:

>Jobs that never crash don't need restart procedures. Jobs that crash every night have
>highly developed restart procedures. When I see elaborate restart procedures, a red flag
>goes up.  It's a symptom of an underlying flaw. 

I see life boats on ships - not because they are likely to sink, but
because the cost of sinking without lifeboats is prohibitive.

Restart procedures (which tend to be simple), simply make sure we
don't update a transaction twice when a job is forced to end early
(for instance when they shut down everything when the power goes
down).

On Nov 16, 5:16 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:

>
> >Excuse me, but nested COPYs would _not_ solve the problem because if
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> files?

No, but if you simply whine on about how it is someone else's (Cobol
designers) fault then the problem will never be solved.

Your 'pre-pre-processor' should only generate new 1st level copybooks
when there is a _change_ in the 2nd level, not just because it has
been regenerated.  Hold a copy of all the 2nd level and compare them.
When there is a difference _then_ you recreate the 1st level.  The new
1st level date will cause all that use it to recompile.

Either that or suck up the 18 hours, I really don't care.

Having multi-level COPY will NOT solve the problem because you will
need to do recurive dependency and if all your 2nd level are
regenerated then it will still compile everything.

"Robert" <no@e.mail> wrote in message 
news:ao5nj3htd2qp1g6p1r7r2c8pvmp8556ul5@4ax.com...
> On Wed, 14 Nov 2007 00:55:56 -0800, Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>
…[84 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I would have severe resevations about using a programming language designed 
by Administrators.... :-)

> Picture a very large shop that runs hundreds of thousands of batch jobs 
> that execute
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> the next step)?

This should be a "write once" exercise by a team of people who know 
something about JCL... It doesn't matter how many millions of programs are 
being used, they get invoked by a Job Control language of some kind, and 
that's where the problem should be addressed.

Your bitch is that with dynamic calling they can't know what will be loaded. 
How is that the fault of the language designers?

Leaving that aside for a moment, there must be approaches that can provide a 
"skeleton"  JCL procedure, or set of  JCL (or equivalent) for ANY program 
that is doing dynamic calling. If you can't know what is being called, then 
you can't pre-check individual load module names/versions, but, you CAN make 
sure that the libraries containing ANYTHING that might be called are on line 
to the job. Shop Programming Standards can ensure that every program logs 
itself and its version when loaded, if that is a concern, so there is an 
audit trail if required. There are always solutions, and they don't 
necessarily involve analysing load modules to obtain symbols...


>
> Unix's ELF standard and the aforementioned ldd (LoaDer Diagnostics) tool 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> programs at execution time? In the vast majority of cases, they don't.

Who are you to decide what COBOL programmers need or want? :-)

The people who designed the language needed to cater for every possible use 
they could reasonably envision. Your site has exceptional needs. 
Nevertheless, the facilites exist to meet even those needs. It is just a 
question of looking at various approaches, and taking a wholistic view; not 
just a language based one.



> A few creative
> programmers will write  'dynamic systems' driven by decision tables (or 
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> module, one in a database, one on his laptop's floppy mounted via NFS.

And that is one reason why installations have Local Standards... To ensure a 
consistent approach.

(Actually, most of the dynamic systems I have worked with or designed, DON'T 
use a list of possible candidates for calling. Instead they CALCULATE the 
name of the needed module. This is possible because the standards require a 
certain naming convention, and program names are carefully controlled and 
allocated in accordance with that convention. A given module name will 
indicate what it does, and which sub-system it applies to...)
>
> This is anarcy.

It is anarchic only if you expect conformance with ONE specific approach...

Flexibility CAN become anarchic if it isn't controlled, that is why it is, 
for the most part, controlled.



> In order to verify programs, I need ALL externals stored in ELF headers.


That is a requirement that is extremely specific and nothing to do with 
COBOL.



> For Cobol programs calling via variables (data-names), I have to extract 
> the VALUE from
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> symbol table. THIS SHOULD NOT BE NECESSARY.

It isn't. You find it necessary because of a specific approach you have 
CHOSEN. A guy as bright and imaginative as you, Robert, could certainly find 
alternative approaches. I reckon you're just not trying... :-)

>If someone on the Cobol Standards Committee
> had known the requirements of LARGE systems, the language would make a 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> thing).

Nothing is Good or Bad, but thinking makes it so.

>
> ------
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> rather than 2 hours.

Very short sighted for a committee to not foresee (over twenty years ago) 
that there would be this "unintended consequence" from what they decided... 
:-)


>Here's why. We don't write copybooks manually, we generate them from
> data dictionaries, for several languages -- .h files for C, .cpy for 
…[41 more quoted lines elided]…
> programs), our builds would already be running in 2 hours.

If you are that bothered by this, you could certainly write your own COPY 
processor. (I just finished doing that for a code generator I delivered a 
few days ago. It's not rocket science... :-))

If you did a hardware upgrade, it wouldn't take 18 hours to build.... :-)

There are always options.

Hmmmmm..... :-)

It's hardly fair to expect a Standards Committee to predict HOW people are 
going to use the provided facilities, before the applications have ever been 
written... :-)

I was interested to see your post, Robert, and I recognise the problems you 
are encountering, but this is not the fault of the people who designed 
COBOL, or even those who later took responsibility for it.

Installations CHOOSE how they are going to do stuff.

It isn't the fault of the language.

Pete.

On Fri, 16 Nov 2007 11:20:25 +1300, "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
wrote:

>
>
…[91 more quoted lines elided]…
>by Administrators.... :-)

A language designed by administrators would make you do all the work, it would decide what
went through and what got rejected. Hmm, maybe they ARE designed by administrators. :)

>> Picture a very large shop that runs hundreds of thousands of batch jobs 
>> that execute
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>that's where the problem should be addressed.

The top level program is named in JCL or script, the child programs it calls are not.

>Your bitch is that with dynamic calling they can't know what will be loaded. 
>How is that the fault of the language designers?

Cobol is the only language that has this problem. Other compiled languages reveal the
names of their children as EXTERNALs. 

>Leaving that aside for a moment, there must be approaches that can provide a 
>"skeleton"  JCL procedure, or set of  JCL (or equivalent) for ANY program 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>to the job.

p: Why didn't you run my job last night?
a: The verifier said Maestro was not available.
p: What the hell is Maestro?
a: Something to do with the Mars Rover.
p: My job runs payroll. It doesn't use Maestro.
a: How are we supposed to know? Maestro is on the library list.
p: What else is on the library list?
a: Thousands of libraries. 
p: The only library I use is a check formatter.
a: See, there you go. We can't run your job until ALL libraries are available.

> Shop Programming Standards can ensure that every program logs 
>itself and its version when loaded, if that is a concern, so there is an 
>audit trail if required. 

The scheduler is capable of logging jobs and programs. It doesn't depend on every program
to "log itself". 

The problem is jobs aborting because of missing programs. Keeping logs does nothing to
address that problem.

>There are always solutions, and they don't 
>necessarily involve analysing load modules to obtain symbols...

Let's send an email to the programmer, asking that he 'verify his job'. If it crashes,
fire him. 

You should be in management. You have a knack for finding alternative solutions that don't
really address the problem, but make us feel vindicated. 

>> Unix's ELF standard and the aforementioned ldd (LoaDer Diagnostics) tool 
>> makes it easy to
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>Who are you to decide what COBOL programmers need or want? :-)

I are one. :)

>The people who designed the language needed to cater for every possible use 
>they could reasonably envision. 

They overlooked very large Unix and mainframe shops. Who would have thunk Cobol would be
used in such places?

>Your site has exceptional needs. 

We run batch jobs that go bump in the night. Is that exceptional?

>Nevertheless, the facilites exist to meet even those needs. It is just a 
>question of looking at various approaches, and taking a wholistic view; not 
>just a language based one.

You forgot to say Think Outside The Box. Have you evr noticed .. people who use that
phrase often are the ones most in need of their own advice.  :)

>> A few creative
>> programmers will write  'dynamic systems' driven by decision tables (or 
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>consistent approach.

We have software acquired from four major companies when we bought them. Each had its own
multiple standards -- one for mainframe, another for Unix, yet another for Java. 

The nice thing about Standards is there are so many to choose from.

>(Actually, most of the dynamic systems I have worked with or designed, DON'T 
>use a list of possible candidates for calling. Instead they CALCULATE the 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>indicate what it does, and which sub-system it applies to...)

Ah, the old Naming Convention Standard is the solution. All we have to do is get the list
of Duly Authorized Programs and make sure they're all present and accounted for.

p: NOW why didn't you run my job?
a: Because program PRFUBAR1xT is missing from the library.
p: The FUBAR system was shut down five years ago.
a: Someone must be using that function. It's still on the list.
p: Can you see who's using it?
a: Here it is. PRONESH8aA needs it.
p: When did it last run?
a: Three years ago. It says On Demand.
p: You held my payroll job because of a three year old one shot program?
a: Hey, I'm just following the rules.

>> This is anarcy.
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>for the most part, controlled.

What about the hundreds of production programs (out of 10,000s) on which none of the
multiple standards was enforced? We tried ignoring them, but they didn't go away. 

>> In order to verify programs, I need ALL externals stored in ELF headers.
>
>That is a requirement that is extremely specific and nothing to do with 
>COBOL.

Yes it does. Cobol programs have many many undeclared EXTERNALs. Other languages have
almost none. The reason is Cobol ENCOURAGES call through data name because of fuzzy
thinking about the meaning of 'dynamic call'. It thinks run-time binding is synonymous
with run-time determination of program name. Other languages require you to declare all
the programs you might call. 

>> For Cobol programs calling via variables (data-names), I have to extract 
>> the VALUE from
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>alternative approaches. I reckon you're just not trying... :-)

On Unix, the only way to call an undeclared EXTERNAL is by calling the OS API function
dlopen() with the program's name. Cobol does this under the covers. Any language can, but
it's rare for the others. I could front-end that function, collecting history in a
database. After a few months, I'd have roughly 98%, and the database would keep getting
better. Seems like a lot of overhead for one language. 

>>If someone on the Cobol Standards Committee
>> had known the requirements of LARGE systems, the language would make a 
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>:-)

Can you name any language or compiler that does NOT support nested include/copy?

RPG doesn't count.

>>Here's why. We don't write copybooks manually, we generate them from
>> data dictionaries, for several languages -- .h files for C, .cpy for 
…[45 more quoted lines elided]…
>few days ago. It's not rocket science... :-))

We DID write our own COPY processor. That's what's causing the problem.

>If you did a hardware upgrade, it wouldn't take 18 hours to build.... :-)
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>written... :-)

In 1985 we had 25 years of mainframe Cobol experience. During the last 10 of those years,
dynamic calls and databases were widely used. 

>I was interested to see your post, Robert, and I recognise the problems you 
>are encountering, but this is not the fault of the people who designed 
>COBOL, or even those who later took responsibility for it.
>
>Installations CHOOSE how they are going to do stuff.

They've chosen Jave this decade.

>It isn't the fault of the language.

This article says the most popular programming languages are Java, C , C++, Visual Basic,
PHP and C#. Cobol finished in 17th place. Why did it go from 1st to 17th? Oh, you already
answered that question. It isn't the fault of the language, it's the fault of Scott
McNealy, Bell Labs, Bill Gates and Zend. 

http://www.itjungle.com/tug/tug121406-story03.html

to load from an internal algorithm, or a raised event, or a menu selection.

>

…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> go away.

Then, it would seem expedient, as I mentioned earlier to have a team of 
people applied to fixing the mess. Given a reluctance to invest in the 
"10,000"s of programs currently in use, one might conclude that you get what 
you deserve...

>
>>> In order to verify programs, I need ALL externals stored in ELF headers.
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> thinking about the meaning of 'dynamic call'.

And you came to this conclusion, exactly....... how?

>It thinks run-time binding is synonymous
> with run-time determination of program name. Other languages require you 
> to declare all
> the programs you might call.

No, they don't. Any program (in ANY Language, and assuming it has the 
requisite Authorization) containing a Shell Exec (for example) can activate 
anything it likes. The same can be done with API calls or DotNET Process 
Objects. The mainframe can do it from COBOL, PL/1, RPG, and Assembler, to my 
knowledge, there are probably others.

It is a tried, proven, and accepted technique. You find  yourself on a 
specific installation which does not seem to be well managed (from what 
you've written; I'm not qualified to judge the reality, as I don't have 
enough information...), you admit to a lack of experience in using this 
facility, and then decide that COBOL is wrong to allow it.

It isn't a good case, Robert.

There is nothing wrong with dynamic linkage OR with dynamically deciding 
what to CALL; your problem is YOUR lack of experience and familiarity with 
it, and the special needs of the site where you are working. Although 
"pre-validating" batch runs may be a useful facility in your case, for most 
sites it doesn't gain them much. The batch runs are pretty standard and run 
from a standard set of JCL or REXX; the required libraries are easily 
identifiable and assignable, and when things crash, it isn't because of a 
system problem like a required load module not being available, but because 
of crap data that has never been encountered before, pushing a program past 
its breaking point.

>
>>> For Cobol programs calling via variables (data-names), I have to extract
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> better. Seems like a lot of overhead for one language.

But it is a requirement. Because that's what they want to do... And the 
solution you just suggested is language independent.


>
>>>If someone on the Cobol Standards Committee
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>>>
<snipped discussion on nested COPY, which we probably all agree would be 
useful...>

> In 1985 we had 25 years of mainframe Cobol experience. During the last 10 
> of those years,
> dynamic calls and databases were widely used.

Ever wondered why? :-)  They are useful facilities.
>
>>I was interested to see your post, Robert, and I recognise the problems 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> They've chosen Jave this decade.

And there are sound reasons for that.

>
>>It isn't the fault of the language.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> PHP and C#. Cobol finished in 17th place.

The same paradigm shift that promoted Java, demoted COBOL. Object Oriented 
programming and component based design, replaced procedural integrated code. 
Java is a "pure" OO language and was designed that way from the beginning. 
C++ allows the ancient C to move easily into the same arena, and even VB has 
been given OO. C# and PHP are both based around the OO paradigm, like Java. 
There are some "new kids" (like Ruby) that are rising quickly also. 
Invariably, they implement the model that COBOL rejected.

Yes, there IS OO COBOL, and I used it and loved it for around 12 years, but 
after using C# there is no comparison. I just finished refactoring and 
upgrading some old COBOL, wrapping it in C# and adding new facilities in C#. 
The new system is written mainly in C# but calls some COBOL components. If 
the entire system had been written in COBOL it would have been around 
100,000 lines of code. Instead it is around 5000 lines of COBOL and 9500 
lines of C#...  (It was enthusuastically received in Melbourne when I 
delivered it two days ago...) Small, efficient, utilising some of the 80,000 
components available through the DotNET framework. Easy and simple to write 
and enhance. There is no way I'd go back to COBOL as a main development 
language.

The point is that it wasn't COBOL's ability to make dynamic CALLs that led 
to the rise of Java...


Why did it go from 1st to 17th? Oh, you already
> answered that question.

Yes. I did. Above.

Pete.

> For example, if there are rules 
>about how libraries get assigned to subsytems, the chaos is diminished. Jobs

>>> Shop Programming Standards can ensure that every program logs
>>>itself and its version when loaded, if that is a concern, so there is an
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>convenient one.

The log contains the name of the failed job and program, but not necessarily the name of
the called program. 

Version control is another aspect. A program can optionally specify the minimum version of
a library in its ELF header. For example, 'foo 3.2 or greater'.  If the library is 3.1,
the bind fails, similar to a missing library. 

>>>Who are you to decide what COBOL programmers need or want? :-)
>>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…

…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>knowledge, there are probably others.

I'm talking about calling programs using the language's standard call mechanism. Calling
via the OS API isn't found in ANY language standard, with the possible exception of
Microsoft. Doing that ties the program to a single OS platform. 

>It is a tried, proven, and accepted technique. You find  yourself on a 
>specific installation which does not seem to be well managed (from what 
>you've written; I'm not qualified to judge the reality, as I don't have 
>enough information...), you admit to a lack of experience in using this 
>facility, and then decide that COBOL is wrong to allow it.

1. Calling the OS API is definately NOT "a tried, proven, and accepted technique" for
calling application programs. The language's call is the accepted technique.
2. My installation is managed better than most. 
3. As a systems programmer, I have extensive experience with the OS API.

>what to CALL; your problem is YOUR lack of experience and familiarity with

down to an Administrative responsibility. For example, if there are rules

running certain systems will only reference components pertinent to those 
systems, and common libraries used for common components. It is manageable, 
but it needs ot be managed. If you DON't manage it, it is hardly fair to

with

> >"pre-validating" batch runs may be a useful facility in your case, for
most
> >sites it doesn't gain them much. The batch runs are pretty standard and
run
> >from a standard set of JCL or REXX; the required libraries are easily
> >identifiable and assignable, and when things crash, it isn't because of a
> >system problem like a required load module not being available, but
because
> >of crap data that has never been encountered before, pushing a program

It isn't I who has the problem, here, Robert.

>
>>Yes, there IS OO COBOL, and I used it and loved it for around 12 years, 
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> And why would you need 80,000 components?

Personally,(so far....), I don't. But if there was a collection of several 
hundred thousand available, I'd still use it. The point is that you never 
know when you may need one, so the richer the collection, the more options 
you have. Anyway, that isn't what's at issue here.

>This now comes
> down to an Administrative responsibility.

Yes, it does. Fortunately, the tool set I have allows me to administer the 
DotNET Framework easily.


>For example, if there are rules
> about how components get assigned to subsytems, the chaos is diminished. 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> Couldn't Fujitsu's COBOL For .NET call the same componnts?

I would think so. But, as they wouldn't sell it to me, I have no way of 
knowing... :-)

Neither can I know which features they have and have not implemented. There 
are C# language facilities which I find very useful and which may not be 
implemented in Fujitsu COBOL for DotNET (automatic iteration with FOREACH is 
one such, reflection and delegation are a couple more, Query Expressions and 
Lambdas as an innate part of the Language are facilities I am currently 
experimenting with and see a future for in my applications...none of these 
things are intrinsic in any version of COBOL...) There are also very easy 
and simple interfaces to allow getting and setting Registry  and Environment 
Values, Standard file and directory dialogs, and so on. None of these are 
directly driven by DotNET, and they are Windows oriented. However, as that 
is my target market, I find them very useful.



> If yes, a Cobol version of your
> C# would measure about the same 9500 lines.

I doubt it; COBOL is much more verbose (however, I agree the system wouldn't 
be 100,000 lines...), but, as there is no way of knowing, I see no point in 
arguing it.

>
>>The point is that it wasn't COBOL's ability to make dynamic CALLs that led
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Oddly enough, I would agree with that statement, but would qualify it by 
saying it was not the ONLY reason for the decline of COBOL.

I don't actually believe COBOL had a downfall. It did what it was supposed 
to do, and still does. That doesn't seem flawed to me.

There is a decline in the use of it, and this decline is accelerating. That 
isn't due to limitations in COBOL; rather, it is because of the fact that 
what it was designed for is of less relevance and has been replaced by a 
different paradigm, and there are now newer languages that fit the new 
paradigm better than COBOL does.

It is certainly true that if the COBOL community had raced to embrace OO 
when it first became available in COBOL, there would be a much stronger 
COBOL user base today than there is, so, to that extent, you can argue that 
"being stuck in the 1980s caused the downfall of COBOL".

Coming back to the point again,...

1. I don't believe there is anything wrong with dynamic calls and dynamic 
module evaluation in COBOL, and certainly nothing that we should castigate 
the designers of it for.

2. I dispute your contention that these are "bad ideas", as my experience 
shows them to be excellent ideas, useful, and allowing a flexibility of 
design and enhancement that is not immediately obvious in some other 
languages.

3. I understand why you have made your protest, and I think there may well 
be a niche for a tool to do what you are looking for, maybe even based 
around a solution you suggested yourself. Our issue is that I don't see it 
as a COBOL shortcoming.

As you are unlikely to change my mind on this, and I am equally unlikely to 
change yours :-) I guess we just have to agree to differ.

I hope you get a satisfactory solution to your problem.

Pete.

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message 
news:gnn3k3tkio3o0r1rh7gd0sitp4nhgvk5p4@4ax.com...
> On Sun, 18 Nov 2007 01:21:48 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> languages.

I believe that, given the size of the COBOL base in the late 1980s, if sites 
were clamouring for OO and had upgraded the way they develop systems to a 
more Object Oriented approach using OO COBOL, there would have been no need 
to move to new OO languages. Although much of the world would still have 
gone to Java and beyond, the sites using COBOL would have had no need to.

Furthermore, COBOL would have had more credibility as a modern OO language, 
just as C moved to C++, and kept the credibility it was already 
establishing.

As more and more COBOL sites moved to component based design (a natural 
progression from OO), it would have been quite normal for COBOL components 
to interact with components in other languages and no-one would have thought 
COBOL any more unusual than Java or C++.

By the time the universal frameworks arrived (DotNET and Mono), there would 
have been intense pressure form the mainframe sites to have these frameworks 
implemented on mainframes, and COBOL would have been playing on this playing 
field just like everyone else. In fact, it is likely that the role of COBOL 
for development of Business components would have been taken as read by the 
entire IT community, and the COBOL base may actually have started to expand 
again. (Obviously, this is debatable and I can't prove it, but it cannot be 
discounted as a possibility....)
>
> That said, why should "the CoBOL community" embrace OO in an existing
> mainframe maintenance shop?    They don't owe anything to the
> language, and change is expensive.

Because NOT changing has left them with no options. COBOL will die within 
the next 8 years and they will HAVE to embrace new technology. If vendors 
stop supporting it (and, my opinion is that they MUST... it gets less and 
less economically viable as the user base erodes; they can't continue 
sinking money into a product that has no future. Watch for acquisitions of 
some current COBOL vendors as they "take the money and run", and eventual 
withdrawal of support from the remaining big players, who will provide 
migration to alternative environments, then phase out COBOL.)

>
> Meanwhile web development occurred elsewhere (which is the way new
> things develop), using cheap tools and people outside of the system.

That was certainly a factor. But it validated the use of objects more than 
any particular language. I have built web sites using JavaScript and COBOL 
CGI. They work, but they are primitive compared to something using Active 
Server Pages, C#, and Ajax.

I don't believe the expansion of the web directly contributed to the 
downfall of COBOL; it certainlly contributed to the general acceptance of 
the OO paradigm, which, if COBOL didn't "keep up"  would have a bad effect 
on the expansion of COBOL. The sad thng about this is that COBOL DID keep 
up; the vendors did a fantastic job of incorporating the new paradigm into 
COBOL. The problem was the user community, which (for the most part) 
received it with resounding silence. No-one was interested in finding out 
why OO would be important. A combination of inertia, conservatism, 
short-sightedness, and even arrogance ("everything I want to do I can do in 
COBOL"), combined with a complete dismissal of the importance of the 
Network, led to strong resistance against OO development methodologies and 
OO COBOL. Now, as people see their jobs being replaced by Java people, there 
is more awareness, but it is too late.

Pete.
```

```
On Fri, 16 Nov 2007 11:27:41 -0800 (PST), Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Nov 16, 5:16 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
>> On Fri, 16 Nov 2007 11:20:25 +1300, "Pete Dashwood" <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz>

>> >How is that the fault of the language designers?
>>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>the same within their own APIs.

Calling an API function is not in any language standard, because it would tie the language
to an OS. Calling through a data name IS in the Cobol language standard. No only does
Cobol make it easy, it confuses some into thinking that type of call is the only or
easiest way to get late binding. They use it as their normal way to call programs. They've
said so in this forum. 

Shop standards should apply the same controls to Cobol 'dynamic calls' as they do to
direct API calls, because dynamic calls DO cause an API call that bypasses some controls. 

>> The problem is jobs aborting because of missing programs. Keeping logs does nothing to
>> address that problem.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>'verification' and call.

The most common reason is a bungled library search path. The file isn't missing from disk.

It's easy to mess up a path in Unix, especially if you use both Windows and Unix. Windows
separates path elements with a semi-colon; Unix uses a colon. A semi-colon in Unix means
end of statement. 

 ld_library_path = ~/lib:$ld_library_path   # is correct
 ld_library_path = ~/lib;$ld_library_path   # sets the path = ~/lib, tries to execute it
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL's Influence on C

_(reply depth: 15)_

- **From:** Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-11-16T22:36:34-08:00

> Calling through a data name IS in the Cobol language standard.

> No only does

so wanted to blame it all on Cobol, language designers, traditionalist

>  ld_library_path = ~/lib:$ld_library_path   # is correct
>  ld_library_path = ~/lib;$ld_library_path   # sets the path = ~/lib, tries to execute it

So your script is wrong, your routine that runs ldd verifies the dlls
are there and then your program fails anyway.  That was a complete
waste of time then.

At least my programs will fail gracefully and log the failure if the
call fails due to the program not existing.

what you claim is the biggest problem, library paths, nor does the

You haven't even established that this is a problem to anyone except
your sloppy third party move to production, and it probably wouldn't
have solved that anyway.

> 2. It slows every call.

Nobody cares.  Once it has loaded from disk the subsequent calls are
via a call table and the difference is insignificant.   The difference
between by dataname and using DLLs is not great anyway, _they_ still
need to be loaded from disk.

> 3. It prevents inventory analysis such as a 'where used' list.

library search path.  Using DLLs won't fix your bungled library search
path. It may allow you to abort the job before the program aborts it
itself when it can't find its library, but this is better in what
way ?

which may use ldd as a small part of this.

> Fail gracefully is an oxymoron. It makes no difference whether you log the error or the
> loader logs it.

Actually it _does_ make a vast difference in my interactive systems.
The system doesn't just pop out of existence, the user gets a message
that the function is unavailable and the rest of the system is still
available. As programs don't disappear this only happens if they try
and do something for the first time, or if it not something they
should be doing anyway.

But regardless, as you say, your 'verification' won't make any
difference at all. The job will still fail, the user still won't get
his report. What was the point of this again ?

```
On Sat, 17 Nov 2007 13:34:54 -0800 (PST), Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Nov 18, 9:37 am, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
>> On Fri, 16 Nov 2007 22:36:34 -0800 (PST), Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote:
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>paths get fixed by changing to dlls.

There is no such thing as a dll on Unix. You should have said shared object (so).

Called programs are all shared objects, whether called by literal or data name."Changing
to dlls" is meaningless. 

>You haven't even established that this is a problem to anyone except
>your sloppy third party move to production, and it probably wouldn't
>have solved that anyway.

I cited half a dozen commercial programs that verify jobs.

Yes, it would have detected the bungled move to production.

>> 2. It slows every call.
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>need to be loaded from disk.

Programs are loaded once, they are called millions of times. Load time is an insignificant
percentage of the program's run time. 

CALL 'literal' does not go through a call table, it goes through a single pointer. 
CALL data-name has to do a string compare, then find its pointer in a call table.
A given CALL data-name might call programs A, B and C, thus have three pointers to choose
from.

>> 3. It prevents inventory analysis such as a 'where used' list.
>
>If you mix everything up so that you need to analyse  program
>executables to get that then your site has more problems than a
>missing executable.

It's the only way to tell from whence a library is called. If you think it can be done
from source code, you're forgetting about third party software for which you have no
source code. And if Cobol programs are getting the names of called programs from menus
(forms), you won't find that in the source code either.

>> 4. It has no advantage when the name is constant, which is usually the case.
>
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
>library search path. 

Wrong. Ldd follows the same logic as the OS program loader. It searches the library path,
checks permissions, calls pvs to check versions. In addition, it can check 'lazy load'
programs, which the OS does not do at program initiation time. It reports ALL problems
that would occur at execution time.

> Using DLLs won't fix your bungled library search
>path. It may allow you to abort the job before the program aborts it
>itself when it can't find its library, but this is better in what
>way ?

Verification identifies a problem hours, if not days, before the job is scheduled to run.
This enables repair ahead of time, so the job will run without error when scheduled.

If all errors could be detected this way, there would never be a production defect.

>The 'hypothetical routine' is the one that you claim will 'verify'
>which may use ldd as a small part of this.

Verification is one part of huge QA systems by BMC, IBM and CA. Companies pay millions of
dollars for functions you say are worthless. Either you're wrong or they are. Given the
ongoing consolidation of small companies into large ones, the real world says big guys are
right and you're wrong.

>> >>  ld_library_path = ~/lib:$ld_library_path   # is correct
>> >>  ld_library_path = ~/lib;$ld_library_path   # sets the path = ~/lib, tries to execute it
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>routine.

It fails the job hours before the program runs, thereby allowing the error to be fixed.

>But regardless, as you say, your 'verification' won't make any
>difference at all. The job will still fail, the user still won't get
>his report. What was the point of this again ?

The job will NOT fail and the user WILL get his report. That's the whole point you refuse
to understand.
```

```
On Nov 18, 3:25 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
> On Sat, 17 Nov 2007 13:34:54 -0800 (PST), Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote:
> >On Nov 18, 9:37 am, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> There is no such thing as a dll on Unix. You should have said shared object (so).

'dll' is an acronym for 'dynamic load library'. Unix .so files (shared
objects) are dynamic load libraries. On Windows .dll files are
(mostly) shared objects too.

You confuse the functionality with the naming convention.

> Called programs are all shared objects, whether called by literal or data name."Changing
> to dlls" is meaningless.

No. Wrong. Not all 'called programs' are 'shared objects'.  For
example with Microfocus the called .int or .gnt is loaded into the
private memory space of the runtime and is not shared with other
runtimes.

But this pedantry on your part is merely to deflect from the fact that
neither of these will fix the bungled load path.

> >You haven't even established that this is a problem to anyone except
> >your sloppy third party move to production, and it probably wouldn't
> >have solved that anyway.
>
> I cited half a dozen commercial programs that verify jobs.

How does this show that others have a problem ?  It may tend to show
that they may use these, or others, to have _solved_ this and thus
_NOT_ have a problem.

> >> 2. It slows every call.
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> percentage of the program's run time.

Whether a call routine is called once or "millions of times" is not a
function of your assertion, but one of how the system is designed and
implemented.

> CALL 'literal' does not go through a call table, it goes through a single pointer.
> CALL data-name has to do a string compare, then find its pointer in a call table.
> A given CALL data-name might call programs A, B and C, thus have three pointers to choose
> from.

And for many, possibly most, systems this is trivial, perhaps even
unmeasurable, compared to the total runtime. Depending of course on
whether your assertion of "millions" has any foundation at all.

Of course if it really is "millions" then there may well be a case for
'copy and paste' into a PERFORMed paragraph to avoid the overhead of
the call.

You may also enjoy reflecting on how modern OO systems do late
binding.

> >> 3. It prevents inventory analysis such as a 'where used' list.
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> (forms), you won't find that in the source code either.

In any rational system the called programs of an application should be
in a set of clearly defined directories that aren't intermixed with
other applications.  It is then only necessary to show that the set in
the directories is complete. Get a manifest from the vendor, or
compare with the proven set on the system test machine.

>
> >> 4. It has no advantage when the name is constant, which is usually the case.
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
> that would occur at execution time.

_ONLY_ if it runs using the same library path as the production job
has.  How do you ensure that the verification has made the same
bungle ?


> Verification identifies a problem hours, if not days, before the job is scheduled to run.
> This enables repair ahead of time, so the job will run without error when scheduled.

How does it check 'hours ahead' that the user won't make a bungle ?


> If all errors could be detected this way, there would never be a production defect.
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> right and you're wrong.

No, Robert, it is _your_ mechanism that I said is worthless. 'your
mechanism' was 'run ldd to find badly specified load paths somewhere
else'.



> >> >>  ld_library_path = ~/lib:$ld_library_path   # is correct
> >> >>  ld_library_path = ~/lib;$ld_library_path   # sets the path = ~/lib, tries to execute it
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> It fails the job hours before the program runs, thereby allowing the error to be fixed.

> >But regardless, as you say, your 'verification' won't make any
> >difference at all. The job will still fail, the user still won't get
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> to understand.

If the problem is that a library or program has
'disappeared' (assuming that it must have been there on the last run),
then why will it not disappear between verification and running ?

Who changes the library load paths ? (oh wait, was that you ?)

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1195079026.976230.183910@o3g2000hsb.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<473AF020.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`
- **References:** `<13iufoh3m3nh7a0@corp.supernews.com> <1194781359.463558.183080@v2g2000hsf.googlegroups.com> <13je1inhintak13@corp.supernews.com> <13je7n9dih2jtdd@corp.supernews.com> <gYHZi.2085$2n.224@bignews8.bellsouth.net> <13jgrs7k1nqet88@corp.supernews.com> <dsmhj317b7236kep9l7j2i53jp39dcvlo6@4ax.com> <Imm_i.4708$LZ7.1327@newsfe15.lga> <snckj3564s4rjul5ctvffht6imaih84m4q@4ax.com> <473AF020.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
On Nov 14, 7:54 pm, "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbr...@efirstbank.com>
wrote:
> >>> On 11/13/2007 at 5:18 PM, in message
> <snckj3564s4rjul5ctvffht6imaih84...@4ax.com>, Robert<n...@e.mail> wrote:
…[77 more quoted lines elided]…
> Frank

The term dynamic can get very confusing at times.  If you use CALL
data-name the call is always dynamic, while if you use CALL literal,
it may or may not be, depending upon your compiler and consequent
linkage editor or binder options.  It has been possible to do dynamic
calls with both CALL literal and CALL data-name from the late 1970s.

The difference in performance between dynamic and static linkage is
insignificant, unless one is also repetitively cancelling dynamically
called program.  One would expect an overall performance improvement
in a multitasking environmnet due to the lack of need for multiple
copies of all the COBOL language subroutines in memory, which should
reduce the amount of paging required.  However, if one wants to run
multiple instances of the same COBOl program at once, then the user
written subroutines can't be in the link pack area (I have a feeling
that is called the RRR (I don't know what it stands for, perhaps
resident routines region?)in DOS/VSE), but they can still be invoked
from the JOBLIB and STEPLIB library specs.

Anyone care to elaborate?
```

```
On Wed, 14 Nov 2007 12:54:56 -0700, "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com>
wrote:

>>>> On 11/13/2007 at 5:18 PM, in message
><snckj3564s4rjul5ctvffht6imaih84m4q@4ax.com>, Robert<no@e.mail> wrote:
…[48 more quoted lines elided]…
>relink of all calling programs.

In the Windows and Unix worlds, dynamic (run time) binding is the standard, as well. 
The format standard for executables and called programs is called Portable Executable (PE)
on Windows, Executable and Linkable Format (ELF) on Unix. Common file extensions for
called programs are .dll and .ocx on Windows, .so and .sl on Unix. 

Static linking is so Old School that Micro Focus dropped support for linker options on the
compiler command line. If you really insist on static, you have to execute the linker as a
separate step. 

The question was: can z/OS or a commonly used tool (not a debuger) verify the existance of
all called program files after compilation and again before execution? If yes, how can it
verify Cobol programs?
```
